<p><b>Robert Francis Kennedy</b> (November 20, 1925 – June 6, 1968) was an American politician and lawyer who served as the 64th <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">United States Attorney General</a> from January 1961 to September 1964, and as a <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senator" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Senator">U.S. Senator</a> from <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> from January 1965 until <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of Robert F. Kennedy">his assassination</a> in June 1968. Kennedy, like his brothers <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Edward</a>, was a prominent member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and has come to be viewed by some historians as an icon of <a href="/wiki/Modern_American_liberalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern American liberalism">modern American liberalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy was born in <a href="/wiki/Brookline,_Massachusetts" title="Brookline, Massachusetts">Brookline, Massachusetts</a>, the seventh child of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Kennedy_Sr." title="Joseph P. Kennedy Sr.">Joseph P. Kennedy Sr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rose_Kennedy" title="Rose Kennedy">Rose Kennedy</a>. After serving in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Naval_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Naval Reserve">U.S. Naval Reserve</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Seaman_apprentice" title="Seaman apprentice">seaman apprentice</a> from 1944 to 1946, Kennedy returned to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> and graduated in 1948. He received his law degree from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia_School_of_Law" title="University of Virginia School of Law">University of Virginia</a>, and was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> bar in 1951. He began his career as a lawyer at the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Justice" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> but later resigned to manage his brother John's successful <a href="/wiki/1952_United_States_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts" title="1952 United States Senate election in Massachusetts">campaign for the U.S. Senate</a> in 1952. The following year, he worked as an assistant counsel to the Senate committee chaired by Senator <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a>. He gained national attention as the chief counsel of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_Labor_Rackets_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate Labor Rackets Committee">Senate Labor Rackets Committee</a> from 1957 to 1959, where he publicly challenged <a href="/wiki/Teamsters" class="mw-redirect" title="Teamsters">Teamsters</a> President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Hoffa" title="Jimmy Hoffa">Jimmy Hoffa</a> over the corrupt practices of the union and authored <i><a href="/wiki/The_Enemy_Within_(Kennedy_book)" title="The Enemy Within (Kennedy book)">The Enemy Within</a></i>, a book about corruption in organized labor. Kennedy resigned from the committee to conduct his brother's campaign in the <a href="/wiki/1960_United_States_presidential_election" title="1960 United States presidential election">1960 presidential election</a>. He was appointed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">United States Attorney General</a> after the successful election and served as the closest advisor to the President from 1961 to 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His tenure is best known for its advocacy for the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a>, the fight against <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">organized crime</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Mafia" title="American Mafia">Mafia</a>, and involvement in U.S. foreign policy related to Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> After his <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">brother's assassination</a>, he remained in office in the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Presidency of Lyndon B. Johnson">Johnson Administration</a> for several months. He left to run for the United States Senate from <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_New_York,_1964" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate election in New York, 1964">New York in 1964</a> and defeated <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> incumbent <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Keating" title="Kenneth Keating">Kenneth Keating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In office, Kennedy opposed racial discrimination and U.S. involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. He was an advocate for issues related to <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a> and formed relationships with <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cesar_Chavez" title="Cesar Chavez">Cesar Chavez</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/1968_United_States_presidential_election" title="1968 United States presidential election">1968</a> Kennedy became a leading candidate for the Democratic nomination for the presidency by appealing to <a href="/wiki/Poor" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor">poor</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latinos_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Latinos in the United States">Hispanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Youth_vote_in_the_United_States" title="Youth vote in the United States">young</a> voters.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> His main challenger in the race was Senator <a href="/wiki/Eugene_McCarthy" title="Eugene McCarthy">Eugene McCarthy</a>. Shortly after winning the <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy_1968_presidential_campaign#California,_South_Dakota,_and_New_Jersey_primaries" title="Robert F. Kennedy 1968 presidential campaign">California primary</a> around midnight on June 5, 1968, Kennedy was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of Robert F. Kennedy">mortally wounded</a> when shot with a pistol by <a href="/wiki/Sirhan_Sirhan" title="Sirhan Sirhan">Sirhan Sirhan</a>, a 24-year-old Palestinian, allegedly in retaliation for his support of Israel following the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a>. Kennedy died the following day. Sirhan was arrested, tried, and convicted, though Kennedy's assassination, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories" title="John F. Kennedy assassination conspiracy theories">like his brother's</a>, continues to be the subject of widespread analysis and <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories" title="Robert F. Kennedy assassination conspiracy theories">numerous conspiracy theories</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Robert Francis Kennedy was born outside Boston in <a href="/wiki/Brookline,_Massachusetts" title="Brookline, Massachusetts">Brookline, Massachusetts</a> on November 20, 1925. He was the seventh of nine children to businessman/politician <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Kennedy_Sr." title="Joseph P. Kennedy Sr.">Joseph P. Kennedy Sr.</a> and philanthropist/socialite <a href="/wiki/Rose_Kennedy" title="Rose Kennedy">Rose Fitzgerald Kennedy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His parents were members of two prominent <a href="/wiki/Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish American">Irish American</a> families in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>. His eight siblings were <a href="/wiki/Joseph_P._Kennedy_Jr." title="Joseph P. Kennedy Jr.">Joseph Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosemary_Kennedy" title="Rosemary Kennedy">Rosemary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Cavendish,_Marchioness_of_Hartington" title="Kathleen Cavendish, Marchioness of Hartington">Kathleen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eunice_Kennedy_Shriver" title="Eunice Kennedy Shriver">Eunice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Kennedy_Lawford" title="Patricia Kennedy Lawford">Patricia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Kennedy_Smith" title="Jean Kennedy Smith">Jean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted</a>. All four of his grandparents were children of Irish immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-JFKlibrary.org_misc_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JFKlibrary.org_misc-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His father was a wealthy businessman and a leading Irish figure in the Democratic Party. After he stepped down as ambassador to the United Kingdom in 1940, Joe Sr. focused his attention on his oldest son, Joseph Jr., expecting that he would enter politics and be elected president. He also urged the younger children to examine and discuss current events in order to propel them to public service.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> After Joseph Jr. was killed during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the senior Kennedy's hopes fell on his second son, John, to become president. Joseph Sr. had the money and connections to play a central role in the family's political ambitions.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TheKennedyFamily1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/TheKennedyFamily1.jpg/250px-TheKennedyFamily1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/TheKennedyFamily1.jpg/375px-TheKennedyFamily1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/TheKennedyFamily1.jpg/500px-TheKennedyFamily1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="602" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TheKennedyFamily1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Kennedy family at <a href="/wiki/Hyannis_Port,_Massachusetts" title="Hyannis Port, Massachusetts">Hyannis Port, Massachusetts</a>, in 1931 with Robert on the bottom left in a jacket</div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy's older brother John was often bedridden by illness and, as a result, became a voracious reader. Although he made little effort to get to know his younger brother during his childhood, John would take him for walks<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas37-40_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas37-40-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and regale him with the stories of heroes and adventures he had read.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty15-16_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty15-16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> One of their favorite authors was <a href="/wiki/John_Buchan" title="John Buchan">John Buchan</a>, who wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Thirty-Nine_Steps" title="The Thirty-Nine Steps">The Thirty-Nine Steps</a></i>, which influenced both Robert and John.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas37-40_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas37-40-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> John sometimes referred to Robert as "Black Robert" due to his prudishness and disposition.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike his older brothers, Kennedy took to heart their mother Rose's agenda for everything to have "a purpose," which included visiting historic sites during family outings, visits to the church during morning walks, and games used to expand vocabulary and math skills.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> He described his position in the family hierarchy by saying, "When you come from that far down, you have to struggle to survive."<sup id="cite_ref-Smith33_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith33-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> As the boys were growing up, he tried frequently to get his older brothers' attention, but was seldom successful.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas37-40_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas37-40-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hilty15-16_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty15-16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1927, the Kennedy family moved to the <a href="/wiki/Riverdale,_Bronx" title="Riverdale, Bronx">Riverdale</a> neighborhood of the Bronx, New York City, and two years later to suburban <a href="/wiki/Bronxville,_New_York" title="Bronxville, New York">Bronxville</a>. During his childhood, Kennedy spent summers and early autumns<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy37_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy37-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> with his family at <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Compound" title="Kennedy Compound">their home</a> (rented in 1926, then purchased in 1929)<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Hyannis_Port,_Massachusetts" title="Hyannis Port, Massachusetts">Hyannis Port, Massachusetts</a>, and Christmas and Easter holidays at their winter retreat in <a href="/wiki/Palm_Beach,_Florida" title="Palm Beach, Florida">Palm Beach, Florida</a>, later purchased in 1933.
</p><p>He attended <a href="/wiki/Riverdale_Country_School" title="Riverdale Country School">Riverdale Country School</a>—a private school for boys—from kindergarten through second grade. He then attended <a href="/wiki/Bronxville_Union_Free_School_District" title="Bronxville Union Free School District">Bronxville Public School</a> in lower Westchester County from third through fifth grade. He repeated the third grade.<sup id="cite_ref-oppenheimer307_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimer307-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A teacher at Bronxville reflected that he was "a regular boy". She added, "It seemed hard for him to finish his work sometimes. But he was only ten after all."<sup id="cite_ref-Smith33_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith33-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hilty23-24_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty23-24-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He then returned to Riverdale Country School for the sixth grade. Kennedy would later recall that during childhood he was "going to different schools, always having to make new friends, and that I was very awkward ... [a]nd I was pretty quiet most of the time. And I didn't mind being alone."<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger21-23_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger21-23-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> He developed an interest in American history. He also decorated his bedroom with pictures of U.S. presidents and filled his bookshelves with volumes on the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. He also became an avid stamp collector and once received a handwritten letter from <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Roosevelt" class="mw-redirect" title="Franklin Roosevelt">Franklin Roosevelt</a>, who was also a philatelist.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith33_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith33-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 1938, Kennedy sailed to <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> with his mother and four youngest siblings to join his father who had begun serving as Ambassador to the United Kingdom. He attended the private Gibbs School for Boys in London for seventh grade. In April 1939, he gave his first public speech at the placing of a cornerstone for a youth club in England. According to embassy and newspaper reports, his statements were pencilled in his own hand and were delivered in a "calm and confident" manner.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Bobby returned to the United States just before the outbreak of World War II in Europe.
</p>
<h3><span id="St._Paul.27s_and_Portsmouth_Priory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="St._Paul's_and_Portsmouth_Priory">St. Paul's and Portsmouth Priory</span></h3>
<p>In September 1939, Kennedy began eighth grade at <a href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_School_(New_Hampshire)" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Paul&#39;s School (New Hampshire)">St. Paul's School</a>, an elite Protestant private <a href="/wiki/University-preparatory_school" class="mw-redirect" title="University-preparatory school">preparatory</a> school for boys in <a href="/wiki/Concord,_New_Hampshire" title="Concord, New Hampshire">Concord, New Hampshire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-RFK:_A_Memoir_&#124;_Jack_Newfield_&#124;_August_27,_2003_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RFK:_A_Memoir_|_Jack_Newfield_|_August_27,_2003-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> that his father favored.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Rose Kennedy was unhappy with the school's use of the Protestant Bible. After two months, she took advantage of her ambassador husband's absence from Boston and withdrew Kennedy from St. Paul's. She enrolled him in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Abbey_School" title="Portsmouth Abbey School">Portsmouth Priory School</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> Catholic boarding school for boys in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_Rhode_Island" title="Portsmouth, Rhode Island">Portsmouth, Rhode Island</a>, which held daily morning and evening prayers and Mass three times a week, with a High Mass on Sundays.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy attended Portsmouth for eighth through tenth grade.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At Portsmouth Priory School, Kennedy was known as "Mrs. Kennedy's little boy Bobby" after he introduced his mother to classmates, who made fun of them. He was defensive of his mother, and on one occasion chased a student out of the dormitory after the boy had commented on her appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas30_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas30-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> He befriended Peter MacLellan and wrote to him, when his brother John was serving in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a>, that he would be visiting his brother "because he might be killed any minute".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy blamed himself when his grades failed to improve. In letters to her son, Rose urged him to read more and to strengthen his vocabulary.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty38_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty38-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Rose also expressed disappointment and wrote that she did not expect him to let her down.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills34_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills34-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> He began developing in other ways, and his brother John noticed his increased physical strength, predicting that the younger Kennedy "would be bouncing me around plenty in two more years".<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty38_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty38-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Monks at Portsmouth Priory School regarded him as a moody and indifferent student. Father Damian Kearney, who was two classes behind Kennedy, reflected that he "didn't look happy" and that he did not "smile much". According to Father Damian's review of school records, Kennedy was a "poor-to-mediocre student, except for history".<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas30_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas30-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Milton_Academy">Milton Academy</span></h3>
<p>In September 1942, Kennedy transferred to his third boarding school, <a href="/wiki/Milton_Academy" title="Milton Academy">Milton Academy</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Milton,_Massachusetts" title="Milton, Massachusetts">Milton, Massachusetts</a>, for eleventh and twelfth grades.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger42_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger42-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> His father, Joseph Kennedy, wanted Kennedy to transfer to Milton, believing it would better prepare his son for Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger42_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger42-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> At Milton, he met and became friends with <a href="/wiki/David_Hackett" title="David Hackett">David Hackett</a>. He invited Hackett to join him for Sunday mass. Hackett started accompanying him, and was impressed when Kennedy took it upon himself to fill in for a missing altar boy one Sunday.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas37-40_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas37-40-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Hackett admired Kennedy's determination to bypass his shortcomings, and remembered him redoubling his efforts whenever something did not come easy to him, which included athletics, studies, success with girls, and popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills34_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills34-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Hackett remembered the two of them being "misfits", a commonality that drew him to Kennedy, along with an unwillingness to conform to how others acted even if doing so meant not being accepted. Kennedy's grades improved.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas37-40_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas37-40-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of his first relationships was with a girl named Piedy Bailey. The pair was photographed together when he walked her home after chapel on a Sunday night. Bailey was fond of him and remembered him as being "very appealing". She recalled him being funny, "separate, larky; outside the cliques; private all the time". Soon after he transferred to Milton, he pressed his father to allow him to enlist, as he wanted to catch up to his brothers who were both serving in the military.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas37-40_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas37-40-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy had arrived at Milton unfamiliar with his peers and made little attempt to know the names of his classmates; he called most of the other boys "fella" instead. For this, he was nicknamed "Fella". Most of the school's students had come in eighth or ninth grade and cliques had already been formed. Despite this, his schoolmates would later say the school had no prejudice. He had an early sense of virtue; he disliked dirty jokes and bullying, once stepping in when an upperclassman tried bothering a younger student.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas37-40_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas37-40-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The headmaster at Milton would later summarize that he was a "very intelligent boy, quiet and shy, but not outstanding, and he left no special mark on Milton".<sup id="cite_ref-Smith33_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith33-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_parents">Relationship with parents</span></h3>
<p>In Kennedy's younger years, his father dubbed him the "runt" of the family and wrote him off. Close family friend <a href="/wiki/Lem_Billings" title="Lem Billings">Lem Billings</a> once remarked to Joe Sr. that he was "the most generous little boy", and Joe Sr. replied that he did not know where his son "got that". Billings commented that the only similarity between Robert and Joe Sr. was their eye color.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas30_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas30-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> As Kennedy grew, his father worried that he was soft on others, conflicting with his ideology. In response, Kennedy developed a tough persona that masked his gentle personality, attempting to appease his father.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills19_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills19-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Biographer Judie Mills wrote that Joe Sr.'s lack of interest in Robert was evident by the length of time it took for him to decide to transfer him to Milton Academy. Both Joe Jr. and John attended the exclusive Protestant prep school <a href="/wiki/Choate_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Choate School">Choate</a> from their first year, while Robert was already a junior by the time he was enrolled at Milton. Despite his father's disdain, Kennedy continued to seek his approval, requesting that Joe Sr. write him a letter about his opinions on different political events and World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills34_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills34-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a child, Kennedy also strove to meet his mother's expectations to become the most dutiful, religious, affectionate, and obedient of the Kennedy children, but the father and son grew distant.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty15-16_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty15-16-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Rose found his gentle personality endearing, though this was noted as having made him "invisible to his father".<sup id="cite_ref-Mills34_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills34-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> She influenced him heavily and like her, he became a devout Catholic and throughout his lifetime he practiced his religion more seriously than the other boys in the family.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> He impressed his parents as a child by taking on a newspaper route, seeking their approval and wishing to distinguish himself. However, he had the family chauffeur driving him in a <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Limited" title="Rolls-Royce Limited">Rolls-Royce</a> so that he could make his deliveries. His mother discovered this and the deliveries ceased.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills19_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills19-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Joe Sr. was satisfied with Kennedy as an adult, believing him to have become "hard as nails", more like him than any of the other children, while his mother believed he exemplified all she had wanted in a child. Mills wrote, "His parents' conflicting views would be echoed in the opinions of millions of people throughout Bobby's life. Robert Kennedy was a ruthless opportunist who would stop at nothing to attain his ambitions. Robert Kennedy was America's most compassionate public figure, the only person who could save a divided country."<sup id="cite_ref-Mills19_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills19-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Naval_service_.281944.E2.80.931946.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_service_(1944–1946)">Naval service (1944–1946)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg/250px-V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg/375px-V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg/500px-V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1355" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:V12-kennedy-910-D-0051.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy (second from left) during his time at <a href="/wiki/Bates_College" title="Bates College">Bates College</a>, in front of a snow replica of a Navy boat.</div></div></div>
<p>Six weeks before his 18th birthday in 1943, Kennedy enlisted in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Reserve" title="United States Navy Reserve">United States Naval Reserve</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Seaman_apprentice" title="Seaman apprentice">seaman apprentice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He was released from active duty in March 1944, when he left Milton Academy early to report to the <a href="/wiki/V-12_Navy_College_Training_Program" title="V-12 Navy College Training Program">V-12 Navy College Training Program</a> at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge, Massachusetts</a>. His V-12 training began at Harvard (March–November 1944) before he was relocated to <a href="/wiki/Bates_College" title="Bates College">Bates College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lewiston,_Maine" title="Lewiston, Maine">Lewiston, Maine</a> (November 1944 – June 1945).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> He returned to Harvard once again in June 1945 completing his post-training requirements in January 1946).<sup id="cite_ref-Walter_Isaacson_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walter_Isaacson-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> At Bates he received a specialized V-12-degree along with 15 others,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and during its <a href="/wiki/Bates_College_traditions" title="Bates College traditions">Winter Carnival</a> built a snow replica of a Navy boat.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:22_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> While in Maine, he wrote a letter to David Hackett in which he expressed feelings of inadequacy and frustration at being isolated from the action. He talked of filling his free time by taking classes with other sailors and remarked that "things are the same as usual up here, and me being my usual moody self I get very sad at times." He added, "If I don't get the hell out of here soon I'll die." Aside from Hackett, who was serving as a paratrooper, more of his <a href="/wiki/Campus_of_Bates_College" title="Campus of Bates College">Parker Hall</a> dorm mates went overseas and left him behind. With others entering combat before him, Kennedy said this made him "feel more and more like a Draft Dodger &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93; or something". He was also frustrated with the apparent desire to shirk military responsibility by some of the other V-12 students.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas44_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas44-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy's brother Joseph P. Kennedy Jr. died in August 1944,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> when his bomber exploded during a volunteer mission known as <a href="/wiki/Operation_Aphrodite" title="Operation Aphrodite">Operation Aphrodite</a>. Robert was most affected by his father's reaction to his eldest son's passing. He appeared completely heartbroken and his peer Fred Garfield commented that Kennedy developed depression and questioned his faith for a short time. After his brother's death, Kennedy gained more attention, moving higher up the family patriarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas44_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas44-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> On December 15, 1945, the <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">U.S. Navy</a> commissioned the <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyer</a> <a href="/wiki/USS_Joseph_P._Kennedy_Jr." title="USS Joseph P. Kennedy Jr.">USS&#160;<i>Joseph P. Kennedy Jr.</i></a>, and shortly thereafter granted Kennedy's request to be released from naval-officer training to serve aboard <i>Kennedy</i> starting on February 1, 1946, as a seaman apprentice on the ship's <a href="/wiki/Shakedown_cruise" title="Shakedown cruise">shakedown cruise</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walter_Isaacson_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walter_Isaacson-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> On May 30, 1946, he received his <a href="/wiki/Honorable_discharge" class="mw-redirect" title="Honorable discharge">honorable discharge</a> from the Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> For his service in the Navy, Kennedy was eligible for the <a href="/wiki/American_Campaign_Medal" title="American Campaign Medal">American Campaign Medal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_Victory_Medal" class="mw-redirect" title="World War II Victory Medal">World War II Victory Medal</a>.
</p>
<h2><span id="Further_study_and_journalism_.281946.E2.80.931951.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_study_and_journalism_(1946–1951)">Further study and journalism (1946–1951)</span></h2>
<p>In September 1946, Kennedy entered Harvard as a junior, having received credit for his time in the V-12 program.<sup id="cite_ref-E.W._Smith_&#124;_2010_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E.W._Smith_|_2010-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> He worked hard to make the <a href="/wiki/Varsity_team" title="Varsity team">varsity</a> <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a> team as an <a href="/wiki/End_(American_football)" class="mw-redirect" title="End (American football)">end</a>; he was a <a href="/wiki/Starting_lineup" title="Starting lineup">starter</a> and scored a <a href="/wiki/Touchdown" title="Touchdown">touchdown</a> in the first game of his senior year before breaking his leg in practice.<sup id="cite_ref-E.W._Smith_&#124;_2010_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E.W._Smith_|_2010-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> He earned his <a href="/wiki/Varsity_letter" title="Varsity letter">varsity letter</a> when his coach sent him in wearing a cast during the last minutes of a game against <a href="/wiki/Yale" class="mw-redirect" title="Yale">Yale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> His father spoke positively of him when he served as a blocking back and sometime receiver for the faster Dave Hackett. Joseph Sr. attended some of Kennedy's practices and saw his son catch a touchdown pass in an early-season rout of Western Maryland. His teammates admired his physical courage. He was five feet ten and 155 pounds, which made him too small for college football. Despite this, he was a fearless hitter and once tackled a 230-pound fullback head-on. Wally Flynn, another player, looked up in the huddle after one play to see him crying after he broke his leg. He disregarded the injury and kept playing.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy earned two varsity letters over the course of the 1946 and 1947 seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout 1946, Kennedy became active in his brother John's campaign for the U.S. Representative seat that was vacated by <a href="/wiki/James_Michael_Curley" title="James Michael Curley">James Curley</a>; he joined the campaign full-time after his naval discharge. Biographer Schlesinger wrote that the election served as an entry into politics for both Robert and John.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Robert graduated from Harvard in 1948 with a bachelor's degree in <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> After graduating, he sailed immediately on the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Queen_Mary" title="RMS Queen Mary">RMS&#160;<i>Queen Mary</i></a> with a college friend for a six-month tour of Europe and the Middle East, accredited as a correspondent for the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston Post">Boston Post</a></i>, filing six stories.<sup id="cite_ref-Ben-David_&#124;_Robert_Kennedy&#39;s_1948_Reports_from_Palestine_&#124;_2008_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ben-David_|_Robert_Kennedy&#39;s_1948_Reports_from_Palestine_|_2008-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Four of these stories, submitted from <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> shortly before the end of the <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">British Mandate</a>, provided <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy%27s_1948_visit_to_Palestine" title="Robert F. Kennedy&#39;s 1948 visit to Palestine">a first-hand view of the tensions</a> in the land.<sup id="cite_ref-Ben-David_&#124;_Robert_Kennedy&#39;s_1948_Reports_from_Palestine_&#124;_2008_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ben-David_|_Robert_Kennedy&#39;s_1948_Reports_from_Palestine_|_2008-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He was critical of British policy on Palestine, and praised the Jewish people he met there calling them "hardy and tough". He held out some hope after seeing Arabs and Jews working side by side but, in the end, feared that the hatred between the groups was too strong and would lead to a war.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1948, he enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia_School_of_Law" title="University of Virginia School of Law">University of Virginia School of Law</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville,_Virginia" title="Charlottesville, Virginia">Charlottesville</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy adapted to this new environment, being elected president of the Student Legal Forum, where he successfully produced outside speakers including <a href="/wiki/James_M._Landis" title="James M. Landis">James M. Landis</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_O._Douglas" title="William O. Douglas">William O. Douglas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Krock" title="Arthur Krock">Arthur Krock</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a> and his family members Joe Sr. and John F. Kennedy. Kennedy's paper on <a href="/wiki/Yalta" title="Yalta">Yalta</a>, written during his senior year, is deposited in the Law Library's Treasure Trove.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> On June 17, 1950, Kennedy married <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Kennedy" title="Ethel Kennedy">Ethel Skakel</a> at St. Mary's Catholic Church in <a href="/wiki/Greenwich,_Connecticut" title="Greenwich, Connecticut">Greenwich, Connecticut</a>. Kennedy graduated from law school in June 1951 and flew with Ethel to Greenwich to stay in his father-in-law's guest house. The couple's first child, <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Kennedy_Townsend" title="Kathleen Kennedy Townsend">Kathleen</a>, was born on July 4, 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> During this time, his brother John tried to keep Joe Sr. "at arm's length". The brothers rarely interacted until Robert was contacted by <a href="/wiki/Kenny_O%27Donnell" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenny O&#39;Donnell">Kenny O'Donnell</a> to repair the relationship between John and their father during John's Senate campaign. As a result of this, Joe Sr. came to view Kennedy favorably as reliable and "willing to sacrifice himself" for the family.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas58_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas58-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 1951, he went to San Francisco as a correspondent for the <i>Boston Post</i> to cover the convention that concluded the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Francisco" title="Treaty of San Francisco">Treaty of Peace with Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In October 1951, he embarked on a seven-week Asian trip with his brother John (then a U.S. Congressman from <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts%27s_11th_congressional_district" title="Massachusetts&#39;s 11th congressional district">Massachusetts' 11th district</a>) and their sister Patricia to Israel, India, Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Because of their age gap, the two brothers had previously seen little of each other—this 25,000-mile (40,000&#160;km) trip came at the behest of their father<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas58_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas58-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and was the first extended time they had spent together, serving to deepen their relationship. On this trip, the brothers met <a href="/wiki/Liaquat_Ali_Khan" title="Liaquat Ali Khan">Liaquat Ali Khan</a> just prior to his death by assassination, and India's prime minister <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Senate_committee_counsel_and_political_campaigns_.281951.E2.80.931960.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Senate_committee_counsel_and_political_campaigns_(1951–1960)">Senate committee counsel and political campaigns (1951–1960)</span></h2>
<h3><span id="JFK_Senate_campaign_and_Joseph_McCarthy_.281952.E2.80.931955.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="JFK_Senate_campaign_and_Joseph_McCarthy_(1952–1955)">JFK Senate campaign and Joseph McCarthy (1952–1955)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:209px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg/207px-Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg" decoding="async" width="207" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg/310px-Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg/413px-Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1631" data-file-height="1784" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert-Ted-John-Kennedy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert, <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">President John F. Kennedy</a> outside the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a> in 1963</div></div></div>
<p>In 1951, Kennedy was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bar_Association" title="Massachusetts Bar Association">Massachusetts Bar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> That November, Kennedy moved with his wife and daughter to a townhouse in the <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Washington,_D.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Georgetown, Washington, D.C.">Georgetown</a> neighborhood of Washington, D.C., and started work as a lawyer in the Internal Security Section of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice_Criminal_Division" title="United States Department of Justice Criminal Division">Criminal Division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">U.S. Department of Justice</a>; the section was charged with investigating suspected Soviet agents.<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy,_Robert_F.,_1951_&#124;_Arthur_J._Morris_Law_Library_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy,_Robert_F.,_1951_|_Arthur_J._Morris_Law_Library-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In February 1952, he was transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_District_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern District of New York">U.S. Attorney's Office</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a> to prosecute fraud cases.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> On June 6, 1952, Kennedy resigned to manage his brother John's successful <a href="/wiki/1952_United_States_Senate_election_in_Massachusetts" title="1952 United States Senate election in Massachusetts">U.S. Senate campaign</a> in <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitman_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitman-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> JFK's victory was of great importance to the Kennedy family, elevating him to national prominence, and turning him into a serious potential presidential candidate. But his brother's victory was equally important to Robert, who felt he had succeeded in eliminating his father's negative perceptions of him.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1952, at the behest of his father, Kennedy was appointed by family friend <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a> as assistant counsel of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Homeland_Security_and_Governmental_Affairs_Permanent_Subcommittee_on_Investigations" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate Homeland Security and Governmental Affairs Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations">U.S. Senate Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-savmappq_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-savmappq-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> However, Kennedy disapproved of the senator's aggressive methods of garnering intelligence on suspected communists.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> This was a highly visible job for him. He resigned in July 1953, but "retained a fondness for McCarthy".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The period of July 1953 to January 1954 saw him at "a professional and personal nadir", feeling that he was adrift while trying to prove himself to the rest of the Kennedy family.<sup id="cite_ref-Goduti16_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goduti16-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After a period as an assistant to his father on the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Commission" title="Hoover Commission">Hoover Commission</a>, Kennedy rejoined the Senate committee staff as chief counsel for the Democratic minority in February 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> That month, McCarthy's chief counsel <a href="/wiki/Roy_Cohn" title="Roy Cohn">Roy Cohn</a> subpoenaed <a href="/wiki/Annie_Lee_Moss" title="Annie Lee Moss">Annie Lee Moss</a>, accusing her of membership in the Communist Party. Kennedy revealed that Cohn had called the wrong Annie Lee Moss and he requested the file on Moss from the FBI. FBI director <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a> had been forewarned by Cohn and denied him access, referring to RFK as "an arrogant whipper-snapper".<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty86_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty86-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> When the Democrats gained the majority in the Senate in January 1955, Kennedy became chief counsel and was a background figure in the televised <a href="/wiki/Army%E2%80%93McCarthy_hearings" title="Army–McCarthy hearings">Army–McCarthy hearings</a> of 1954 into McCarthy's conduct.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The Annie Lee Moss incident turned Cohn into an enemy, which led to Kennedy assisting Democratic senators in ridiculing Cohn during the hearings. The animosity grew to the point where Cohn had to be restrained after asking RFK if he wanted to fight him.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty86_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty86-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> For his work on the McCarthy committee, Kennedy was included in a list of Ten Outstanding Young Men of 1954, created by the U.S. Junior Chamber of Commerce. His father had arranged the nomination, his first national award.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty90_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty90-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In 1955 Kennedy was admitted to practice before the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Stevenson_aide_and_focus_on_organized_labor_.281956.E2.80.931960.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Stevenson_aide_and_focus_on_organized_labor_(1956–1960)">Stevenson aide and focus on organized labor (1956–1960)</span></h3>
<p>In 1956, Kennedy moved his growing family outside Washington to a house called <a href="/wiki/Hickory_Hill_(McLean,_Virginia)" title="Hickory Hill (McLean, Virginia)">Hickory Hill</a>, which he purchased from his brother John. This enormous 13-bedroom, 13-bath home was situated on 6 acres (2.4&#160;ha) in <a href="/wiki/McLean,_Virginia" title="McLean, Virginia">McLean, Virginia</a>. Kennedy went on to work as an aide to <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a> during the <a href="/wiki/1956_United_States_presidential_election" title="1956 United States presidential election">1956 presidential election</a> which helped him learn how national campaigns worked, in preparation for a future run by his brother, Jack.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Unimpressed with Stevenson, he reportedly voted for incumbent <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-leamer2001_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leamer2001-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was also a delegate at the <a href="/wiki/1956_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1956 Democratic National Convention">1956 Democratic National Convention</a>, having replaced <a href="/wiki/Tip_O%27Neil" class="mw-redirect" title="Tip O&#39;Neil">Tip O'Neil</a> at the request of his brother John, joining in what was ultimately an unsuccessful effort to help JFK get the vice presidential nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after this, following instructions by his father, Kennedy tried making amends with J. Edgar Hoover.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> There seemed to be some improvement in their interactions, which came to be seen as "elemental political necessity" by Kennedy. This later changed after Kennedy was appointed attorney general, where Hoover saw him as an "unprecedented threat".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1957 to 1959, he made a name for himself while serving as the chief counsel to the Senate's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Select_Committee_on_Improper_Activities_in_Labor_and_Management" title="United States Senate Select Committee on Improper Activities in Labor and Management">McClellan Committee</a> under chairman <a href="/wiki/John_L._McClellan" title="John L. McClellan">John L. McClellan</a>. Kennedy was given authority over testimony scheduling, areas of investigation, and witness questioning by McClellan, a move that was made by the chairman to limit attention to himself and allow outrage by organized labor to be directed toward Kennedy.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In a famous scene, Kennedy squared off with <a href="/wiki/International_Brotherhood_of_Teamsters" title="International Brotherhood of Teamsters">Teamsters Union</a> President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Hoffa" title="Jimmy Hoffa">Jimmy Hoffa</a> during the antagonistic argument that marked Hoffa's testimony.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> During the hearings, Kennedy received criticism from liberal critics and other commentators both for his outburst of impassioned anger and doubts about the innocence of those who invoked the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifth Amendment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Senators <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Mundt" class="mw-redirect" title="Karl Mundt">Karl Mundt</a> wrote to each other and complained about "the Kennedy boys" having hijacked the McClellan Committee by their focus on Hoffa and the Teamsters. They believed Kennedy covered for <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reuther" title="Walter Reuther">Walter Reuther</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers" title="United Automobile Workers">United Automobile Workers</a>, a union which typically would back Democratic office seekers. Amidst the allegations, Kennedy wrote in his journal that the two senators had "no guts" as they never addressed him directly, only through the press.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> He left the committee in late 1959 in order to run his brother's presidential campaign.
</p>
<h3><span id="JFK_presidential_campaign_.281960.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="JFK_presidential_campaign_(1960)">JFK presidential campaign (1960)</span></h3>
<p>In 1960 Kennedy published the successful book, which he had drafted over the summer of the previous year, <i>The Enemy Within</i>, describing the corrupt practices within the Teamsters and other unions that he had helped investigate. Biographer Evan Thomas wrote that the book was a bestseller and could have launched a political career on its own, but "family duty called",<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas88_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas88-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and Kennedy went to work on the presidential campaign of his brother, John.  In contrast to his role in his brother's previous campaign eight years prior, Kennedy gave stump speeches throughout the primary season, gaining confidence as time went on.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> His strategy "to win at any cost" led him to call on <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt Jr.">Franklin D. Roosevelt Jr.</a> to attack <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a> as a draft dodger; Roosevelt eventually did make the statement that Humphrey avoided service.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerned that John Kennedy was going to receive the Democratic Party's nomination, some supporters of Lyndon Johnson, who was also running for the nomination, revealed to the press that JFK had <a href="/wiki/Addison%27s_disease" title="Addison&#39;s disease">Addison's disease</a>, saying that he required life-sustaining cortisone treatments. Though in fact a diagnosis had been made, Kennedy tried to protect his brother by denying the allegation, saying that JFK had never had "an ailment described classically as Addison's disease".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> After securing the nomination, John Kennedy nonetheless decided to offer Lyndon Johnson the vice presidency. This did not sit well with some Kennedy supporters, and Robert tried unsuccessfully to convince Johnson to turn down the offer, leading him to view Robert with contempt afterward.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> RFK had already disliked Johnson prior to the presidential campaign, seeing him as a threat to his brother's ambitions.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> RFK wanted his brother to choose labor leader <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reuther" title="Walter Reuther">Walter Reuther</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Despite Kennedy's attempts, Johnson became his brother's running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy worked toward downplaying his brother's Catholic faith during the primary but took a more aggressive and supportive stance during the general election. These concerns were mostly calmed after JFK delivered a speech in September in Houston where he said that he was in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The following month, Kennedy was involved in securing the release of civil rights leader <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> from a jail in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>. Kennedy spoke with Georgia governor <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Vandiver" title="Ernest Vandiver">Ernest Vandiver</a> and later, Judge Oscar Mitchell, after the judge had sentenced King for violating his probation when he protested at a whites-only snack bar.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Attorney_General_of_the_United_States_.281961.E2.80.931964.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Attorney_General_of_the_United_States_(1961–1964)">Attorney General of the United States (1961–1964)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg/220px-Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="363" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg/330px-Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg/440px-Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg 2x" data-file-width="621" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Attorney_General_Robert_Kennedy_testifying_before_a_Senate_subcommittee_hearing_on_crime.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy testifies before the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Government_Operations" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate Committee on Government Operations">Senate Committee on Government Operations</a>' <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Subcommittee_on_Investigations" class="mw-redirect" title="Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations">Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations</a> about organized crime, September 1963</div></div></div>
<p>After winning the 1960 presidential election, President-elect John F. Kennedy appointed his younger brother attorney general. The choice was controversial, with publications including <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> calling him inexperienced and unqualified.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> He had no experience in any state or federal court,<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> causing the president to joke, "I can't see that it's wrong to give him a little legal experience before he goes out to practice law."<sup id="cite_ref-All_He_Asked_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-All_He_Asked-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> However, Kennedy was hardly a novice as a lawyer, having gained significant experience conducting investigations and questioning witnesses as a Justice Department attorney and Senate committee counsel and staff director.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Baker" title="Bobby Baker">Bobby Baker</a>, the Senate majority secretary and a protégé of Lyndon Johnson, President-elect Kennedy did not want to name his brother attorney general. However, their father overruled the president-elect. At the behest of Johnson, Baker persuaded the influential Southern senator <a href="/wiki/Richard_Russell_Jr." title="Richard Russell Jr.">Richard Russell</a> to allow a voice vote to confirm the president's brother in January 1961, as Kennedy "would have been lucky to get 40 votes" on a roll-call vote.<sup id="cite_ref-BobbyBaker_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BobbyBaker-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy performed well in his confirmation hearing and chose what friend and biographer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a> called an "outstanding" group of deputy and assistant attorneys general, including <a href="/wiki/Byron_White" title="Byron White">Byron White</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Katzenbach" title="Nicholas Katzenbach">Nicholas Katzenbach</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Author James W. Hilty concludes that Kennedy "played an unusual combination of roles—campaign director, attorney general, executive overseer, controller of patronage, chief adviser, and brother protector" and that nobody before him had had such power.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> His tenure as attorney general was easily the period of greatest power for the office&#160;– no previous United States attorney general had enjoyed such clear influence on all areas of policy during an administration.<sup id="cite_ref-James_W._Hilty_2000_408_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James_W._Hilty_2000_408-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> To a great extent, President Kennedy sought the advice and counsel of his younger brother, with Robert being the president's closest political adviser. He was relied upon as both the president's primary source of administrative information, and as a general counsel with whom trust was implicit. He exercised widespread authority over every cabinet department, leading the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> to dub him "Bobby—Washington's No. 2-man".<sup id="cite_ref-James_W._Hilty_2000_408_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James_W._Hilty_2000_408-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The president once remarked about his brother, "If I want something done and done immediately I rely on the Attorney General. He is very much the doer in this administration, and has an organizational gift I have rarely if ever seen surpassed."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Berlin">Berlin</span></h3>
<p>As one of the president's closest White House advisers, Kennedy played a crucial role in the events surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Crisis_of_1961" title="Berlin Crisis of 1961">Berlin Crisis of 1961</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Operating mainly through a private <a href="/wiki/Backchannel" title="Backchannel">backchannel</a> connection to Soviet spy <a href="/wiki/Georgi_Bolshakov" title="Georgi Bolshakov">Georgi Bolshakov</a>, he relayed important diplomatic communications between the American and Soviet governments.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Most significantly, this connection helped the U.S. set up the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Summit" class="mw-redirect" title="Vienna Summit">Vienna Summit</a> in June 1961, and later to defuse the tank standoff with the Soviets at Berlin's <a href="/wiki/Checkpoint_Charlie" title="Checkpoint Charlie">Checkpoint Charlie</a> in October.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organized_crime_and_the_Teamsters">Organized crime and the Teamsters</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg/220px-Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg/330px-Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg/440px-Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="464" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hoover_Kennedys_1961_by_Abbie_Rowe.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John F. Kennedy, J. Edgar Hoover, and Kennedy in 1961</div></div></div>
<p>As attorney general, Kennedy pursued a relentless crusade against <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">organized crime</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Mafia" title="American Mafia">Mafia</a>, sometimes disagreeing on strategy with <a href="/wiki/Director_of_the_Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Director of the Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI Director</a> <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a>. Convictions against organized crime figures rose by 800 percent during his term.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy worked to shift Hoover's focus away from communism, which Hoover saw as a more serious threat, to organized crime. According to <a href="/wiki/James_Neff" title="James Neff">James Neff</a>, Kennedy's success in this endeavor was due to his brother's position, giving the attorney general leverage over Hoover.<sup id="cite_ref-npr2015_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr2015-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Biographer <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hack" title="Richard Hack">Richard Hack</a> concluded that Hoover's dislike for Kennedy came from his being unable to control him.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He was relentless in his pursuit of <a href="/wiki/International_Brotherhood_of_Teamsters" title="International Brotherhood of Teamsters">Teamsters Union</a> president <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Hoffa" title="Jimmy Hoffa">Jimmy Hoffa</a>, due to Hoffa's known corruption in financial and electoral matters, both personally and organizationally.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The enmity between the two men was intense, with accusations of a personal vendetta – what Hoffa called a "blood feud" – exchanged between them.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> On July 7, 1961, after Hoffa was reelected to the Teamsters presidency, RFK told reporters the government's case against Hoffa had not been changed by what he called "a small group of teamsters" supporting him.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The following year, it was leaked that Hoffa had claimed to a Teamster local that Kennedy had been "bodily" removed from his office, the statement being confirmed by a Teamster press agent and Hoffa saying Kennedy had only been ejected.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> 
In 1964 Hoffa was imprisoned for jury tampering.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> After learning of Hoffa's conviction by telephone, Kennedy issued congratulatory messages to the three prosecutors.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span></h3>
<p>Kennedy expressed the administration's commitment to civil rights during a 1961 <a href="/wiki/Law_Day_Address" title="Law Day Address">speech</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia_Law_School" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Georgia Law School">University of Georgia Law School</a>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We will not stand by or be aloof—we will move. I happen to believe that <a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">the 1954 decision</a> was right. But my belief does not matter. It is now the law. Some of you may believe the decision was wrong. That does not matter. It is the law.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg/220px-Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="335" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg/330px-Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg/440px-Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3239" data-file-height="4936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Kennedy_CORE_rally_speech2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy speaking to civil rights demonstrators in front of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> on June 14, 1963</div></div></div>
<p>FBI Director J. Edgar Hoover viewed civil rights leader Martin Luther King Jr. as an upstart troublemaker,<sup id="cite_ref-american_public_radio_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-american_public_radio-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> calling him an "enemy of the state".<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In February 1962<sup id="cite_ref-lllkkaf_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lllkkaf-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Hoover presented Kennedy with allegations that some of King's close confidants and advisers were <a href="/wiki/Communist#Cold_War_years" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">communists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ippppafab_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ippppafab-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Concerned about the allegations, the FBI deployed agents to monitor King in the following months.<sup id="cite_ref-lllkkaf_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lllkkaf-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy warned King to discontinue the suspected associations. In response, King agreed to ask suspected Communist <a href="/wiki/Jack_O%27Dell" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack O&#39;Dell">Jack O'Dell</a> to resign from the SCLC, but refused to heed to the request to ask <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Levison" title="Stanley Levison">Stanley Levison</a>, whom he regarded as a trusted advisor, to resign.<sup id="cite_ref-rking_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rking-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In October 1963,<sup id="cite_ref-rking_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rking-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy issued a written directive authorizing the FBI to wiretap King and other leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">Southern Christian Leadership Conference</a>, King's civil rights organization.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_41_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_41-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Although Kennedy only gave written approval for limited wiretapping of King's phones "on a trial basis, for a month or so",<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Hoover extended the clearance so that his men were "unshackled" to look for evidence in any areas of King's life they deemed worthy.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The wiretapping continued through June 1966 and was revealed in 1968, days before Kennedy's death.<sup id="cite_ref-the_atlantic_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_atlantic-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy remained committed to civil rights enforcement to such a degree that he commented in 1962 that it seemed to envelop almost every area of his public and private life, from prosecuting corrupt Southern electoral officials to answering late night calls from <a href="/wiki/Coretta_Scott_King" title="Coretta Scott King">Coretta Scott King</a> concerning the imprisonment of her husband for demonstrations in <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> During his tenure as attorney general, he undertook the most energetic and persistent desegregation of the administration that Capitol Hill had ever experienced. He demanded that every area of government begin recruiting realistic levels of black and other ethnic workers, going so far as to criticize Vice President Johnson for his failure to desegregate his own office staff.  However, relations between the Kennedys and civil rights activists could be tense, partly due to the administration's decision that a number of complaints which King filed with the Justice Department between 1961 and 1963 be handled "through negotiation between the city commission and Negro citizens."<sup id="cite_ref-rking_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rking-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although it has become commonplace to assert the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Presidency of John F. Kennedy">The Kennedy Administration</a>" or even "President Kennedy" when discussing the legislative and executive support of the civil rights movement, between 1960 and 1963 a great many of the initiatives that occurred during his tenure were the result of the passion and determination of an emboldened Robert Kennedy, who, through his rapid education in the realities of Southern racism, underwent a thorough conversion of purpose as attorney general. Asked in an interview in May 1962, "What do you see as the big problem ahead for you, is it crime or internal security?" Kennedy replied, "Civil rights."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The president came to share his brother's sense of urgency on the matters at hand to such an extent that it was at the attorney general's insistence that he made his famous <a href="/wiki/Report_to_the_American_People_on_Civil_Rights" title="Report to the American People on Civil Rights">June 1963 address to the nation on civil rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg/240px-RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg/360px-RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg/480px-RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="726" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RFK_and_MLK_together.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Martin Luther King Jr. and Kennedy in Washington, D.C. on June 22, 1963</div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy played a large role in the response to the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Riders" title="Freedom Riders">Freedom Riders</a> protests. He acted after the <a href="/wiki/Anniston,_Alabama#The_Civil_Rights_era" title="Anniston, Alabama">Anniston bus bombings</a> to protect the Riders in continuing their journey, sending <a href="/wiki/John_Seigenthaler" title="John Seigenthaler">John Seigenthaler</a>, his administrative assistant, to Alabama to attempt to secure the Riders' safety there. Despite a <a href="/wiki/Work_rule" title="Work rule">work rule</a> which allowed a driver to decline an assignment which he regarded as a potentially unsafe one, he persuaded a manager of <a href="/wiki/The_Greyhound_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="The Greyhound Corporation">The Greyhound Corporation</a> to obtain a coach operator who was willing to drive a special bus for the continuance of the Freedom Ride from Birmingham, Alabama, to Montgomery, Alabama, on the circuitous journey to Jackson, Mississippi.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later, during the attack and burning by a white mob of the First Baptist Church in Montgomery, at which Martin Luther King Jr. and some 1,500 sympathizers were in attendance, the attorney general telephoned King to ask for his assurance that they would not leave the building until the force of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marshals_Service" title="United States Marshals Service">U.S. Marshals</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="National Guard of the United States">National Guard</a> he sent had secured the area. King proceeded to berate Kennedy for "allowing the situation to continue".
