King later publicly thanked him for dispatching the forces to break up the attack that might otherwise have ended his life.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy then negotiated the safe passage of the Freedom Riders from the First Baptist Church to Jackson, Mississippi, where they were arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> He offered to bail the Freedom Riders out of jail, but they refused, which upset him, leading him to call any bandwagoners of the original freedom rides "honkers".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Kennedy's attempts to end the Freedom Rides early were tied to an upcoming summit with <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>. He believed the continued international publicity of race riots would tarnish the president heading into international negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> This attempt to curtail the Freedom Rides alienated many of the civil rights leaders who, at the time, perceived him as intolerant and narrow-minded.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> In an attempt to better understand and improve race relations, Kennedy held a <a href="/wiki/Baldwin%E2%80%93Kennedy_meeting" title="Baldwin–Kennedy meeting">private meeting in New York City in May 1963</a> with a black delegation coordinated by prominent author <a href="/wiki/James_Baldwin" title="James Baldwin">James Baldwin</a>.
</p><p>In September 1962, Kennedy sent U.S. marshals to <a href="/wiki/Oxford,_Mississippi" title="Oxford, Mississippi">Oxford, Mississippi</a>, to enforce a federal court order allowing the admittance of the first African-American student, <a href="/wiki/James_Meredith" title="James Meredith">James Meredith</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mississippi" title="University of Mississippi">University of Mississippi</a>. The attorney general had hoped that legal means, along with the escort of U.S. marshals, would be enough to force Governor <a href="/wiki/Ross_Barnett" title="Ross Barnett">Ross Barnett</a> to allow Meredith's admission. He also was very concerned there might be a "mini-civil war" between U.S. Army troops and armed protesters.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> President Kennedy reluctantly sent federal troops after the situation on campus turned violent.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ensuing <a href="/wiki/Ole_Miss_riot_of_1962" title="Ole Miss riot of 1962">riots during the period of Meredith's admittance</a> resulted in hundreds of injuries and two deaths,<sup id="cite_ref-npr_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> yet Kennedy remained adamant that black students had the right to enjoy the benefits of all levels of the educational system. The Office of Civil Rights also hired its first African-American lawyer<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (October 2017)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and began to work cautiously with leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Kennedy saw voting as the key to racial justice and collaborated with presidents Kennedy and Johnson to create the landmark <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a>, which helped bring an end to <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow_laws" title="Jim Crow laws">Jim Crow laws</a>. Between December 1961 and December 1963, Kennedy also expanded the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice_Civil_Rights_Division" title="United States Department of Justice Civil Rights Division">United States Department of Justice Civil Rights Division</a> by 60 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Steel">U.S. Steel</span></h3>
<p>At the direction of the president, Kennedy also used the power of federal agencies to influence <a href="/wiki/U.S._Steel" title="U.S. Steel">U.S. Steel</a> not to institute a price increase.<sup id="cite_ref-time_steel_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_steel-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> wrote that the administration had set prices of steel "by naked power, by threats, by agents of the state security police."<sup id="cite_ref-jfk_bio_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfk_bio-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Yale law professor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Reich" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Reich">Charles Reich</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> that the Justice Department had violated <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a> by calling a federal grand jury to indict U.S. Steel so quickly, then disbanding it after the price increase did not occur.<sup id="cite_ref-jfk_bio_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jfk_bio-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_penalty_issues">Death penalty issues</span></h3>
<p>During the Kennedy administration, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal government</a> carried out its last pre-<a href="/wiki/Furman_v._Georgia" title="Furman v. Georgia"><i>Furman</i></a> federal execution (of <a href="/wiki/Victor_Feguer" title="Victor Feguer">Victor Feguer</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, 1963),<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> and Kennedy, as attorney general, represented the government in this case.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1967 Kennedy expressed his strong willingness to support a bill then under consideration for the abolition of the death penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuba">Cuba</span></h3>
<p>As his brother's confidant, Kennedy oversaw the CIA's anti-<a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Castro</a> activities after the failed <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a>. He also helped develop the strategy during the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> to blockade Cuba instead of initiating a military strike that might have led to nuclear war. He had initially been among the more hawkish members of the administration on matters concerning Cuban insurrectionist aid. His initial strong support for covert actions in Cuba soon changed to a position of removal from further involvement once he became aware of the CIA's tendency to draw out initiatives, and provide itself with almost unchecked authority in matters of foreign covert operations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy,_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House,_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy%2C_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House%2C_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg/220px-President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy%2C_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House%2C_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy%2C_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House%2C_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg/330px-President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy%2C_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House%2C_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy%2C_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House%2C_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg/440px-President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy%2C_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House%2C_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2358" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_with_Attorney_General._President_Kennedy,_Attorney_General_Kennedy._White_House,_Oval_Office_Doorway._-_NARA_-_194221.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President Kennedy with his brother Robert, 1963</div></div></div>
<p>Allegations that the Kennedys knew of plans by the CIA to kill <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>, or approved of such plans, have been debated by historians over the years. JFK's friend and associate, historian <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a>, for example, expressed the opinion that operatives linked to the CIA were among the most reckless individuals to have operated during the period—providing themselves with unscrutinized freedoms to threaten the lives of Castro and other members of the Cuban revolutionary government regardless of the legislative apparatus in Washington—freedoms that, unbeknownst to those at the White House attempting to prevent a nuclear war, placed the entire U.S.–Soviet relationship in perilous danger.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The "<a href="/wiki/Family_Jewels_(Central_Intelligence_Agency)" title="Family Jewels (Central Intelligence Agency)">Family Jewels</a>" documents, declassified by the CIA in 2007, suggest that before the Bay of Pigs Invasion, the attorney general personally authorized one such assassination attempt.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> However, ample evidence exists to the contrary, specifically that Kennedy was only informed of an earlier plot involving the CIA's use of Mafia bosses <a href="/wiki/Santo_Trafficante_Jr." title="Santo Trafficante Jr.">Santo Trafficante Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Roselli" title="John Roselli">John Roselli</a> during a briefing on May 7, 1962, and in fact directed the CIA to halt any existing efforts directed at Castro's assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Concurrently, Kennedy served as the president's personal representative in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Mongoose" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Mongoose">Operation Mongoose</a>, the post-Bay of Pigs covert operations program established in November 1961 by the president.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Mongoose was meant to incite a revolution within Cuba that would result in the downfall of Castro, not Castro's assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Cuban Missile Crisis, Kennedy proved himself to be a gifted politician with an ability to obtain compromises, tempering aggressive positions of key figures in the hawk camp. The trust the President placed in him on matters of negotiation was such that his role in the crisis is today seen as having been of vital importance in securing a blockade, which averted a full military engagement between the United States and Soviet Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-Hayes_2019_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes_2019-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> His clandestine meetings with members of the Soviet Government continued to provide a key link to Nikita Khrushchev during even the darkest moments of the Crisis, in which the threat of nuclear strikes was considered a very present reality.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> On the last night of the crisis, President Kennedy was so grateful for his brother's work in averting nuclear war that he summed it up by saying, "Thank God for Bobby."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination_of_President_John_F._Kennedy">Assassination of President John F. Kennedy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral,_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg/240px-JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg/360px-JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg/480px-JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral%2C_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2954" data-file-height="2336" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK%27s_family_leaves_Capitol_after_his_funeral,_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert Kennedy at the funeral of his brother, President John F. Kennedy, November 25, 1963</div></div></div>
<p>At the time that President Kennedy <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">was assassinated</a> in Dallas on November 22, 1963, RFK was at home with aides from the Justice Department. J. Edgar Hoover called and told him his brother had been shot.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmero1-6_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmero1-6-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Hoover then hung up before he could ask any questions. Kennedy later said he thought Hoover had enjoyed telling him the news.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy then received a call from Tazewell Shepard, a naval aide to the president, who told him that his brother was dead.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmero1-6_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmero1-6-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after the call from Hoover, Kennedy phoned <a href="/wiki/McGeorge_Bundy" title="McGeorge Bundy">McGeorge Bundy</a> at the White House, instructing him to change the locks on the president's files. He ordered the Secret Service to dismantle the Oval Office and cabinet room's secret taping systems. He scheduled a meeting with CIA director <a href="/wiki/John_McCone" class="mw-redirect" title="John McCone">John McCone</a> and asked if the CIA had any involvement in his brother's death. McCone denied it, with Kennedy later telling investigator Walter Sheridan that he asked the director "in a way that he couldn't lie to me, and they [the CIA] hadn't".<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An hour after the president was shot, Bobby Kennedy received a phone call from Vice President Johnson before Johnson boarded <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a>. RFK remembered their conversation starting with Johnson demonstrating sympathy before the vice president stated his belief that he should be sworn in immediately; RFK opposed the idea since he felt "it would be nice" for President Kennedy's body to return to Washington with the deceased president still being the incumbent.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Eventually, the two concluded that the best course of action would be for Johnson to take the oath of office before returning to Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In his 1971 book <i>We Band of Brothers</i>, aide <a href="/wiki/Edwin_O._Guthman" title="Edwin O. Guthman">Edwin O. Guthman</a> recounted Kennedy admitting to him an hour after receiving word of his brother's death that he thought he would be the one "they would get" as opposed to his brother.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In the days following the assassination, he wrote letters to his two eldest children, Kathleen and Joseph, saying that as the oldest Kennedy family members of their generation, they had a special responsibility to remember what their uncle had started and to love and serve their country.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> He was originally opposed to Jacqueline Kennedy's decision to have a closed casket, as he wanted the funeral to keep with tradition, but he changed his mind after seeing the cosmetic, waxen remains.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy was asked by <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> leaders to introduce a film about his late brother at <a href="/wiki/1964_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1964 Democratic National Convention">the 1964 party convention</a>. When he was introduced, the crowd, including party bosses, elected officials, and delegates, applauded thunderously and tearfully for a full 22 minutes before they would let him speak.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> He was close to breaking down before he spoke about his brother's vision for both the party and the nation and recited a quote from Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i> (3.2) that Jacqueline had given him:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>When [he] shall die<br />
Take him and cut him out in little stars,<br />
And he will make the face of heaven so fine<br />
That all the world will be in love with night<br />
And pay no worship to the garish sun.
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>The ten-month investigation by the <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a> of 1963–1964 concluded that the president had been assassinated by <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a> and that Oswald had acted alone. On September 27, 1964, Kennedy issued a statement through his New York campaign office: "As I said in Poland last summer, I am convinced Oswald was solely responsible for what happened and that he did not have any outside help or assistance. He was a malcontent who could not get along here or in the Soviet Union."<sup id="cite_ref-Pittsburgh_Post-Gazette;_September_28,_1964_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pittsburgh_Post-Gazette;_September_28,_1964-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> He added, "I have not read the report, nor do I intend to. But I have been briefed on it and I am completely satisfied that the Commission investigated every lead and examined every piece of evidence. The Commission's inquiry was thorough and conscientious."<sup id="cite_ref-Pittsburgh_Post-Gazette;_September_28,_1964_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pittsburgh_Post-Gazette;_September_28,_1964-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> After a meeting with Kennedy in 1966, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a> wrote: "It is evident that he believes that [the Warren Commission's report] was a poor job and will not endorse it, but that he is unwilling to criticize it and thereby reopen the whole tragic business."<sup id="cite_ref-Caro_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caro-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Jerry Bruno, an "<a href="/wiki/Advance_man" class="mw-redirect" title="Advance man">advance man</a>" for JFK who also worked on RFK's 1968 presidential campaign, would later state in 1993: "I talked to Robert Kennedy many times about the Warren Commission, and he never doubted their result."<sup id="cite_ref-Bangor_Daily_News;_December_16,_1993_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bangor_Daily_News;_December_16,_1993-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> In a 2013 interview with <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> journalist <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Rose" title="Charlie Rose">Charlie Rose</a>, son <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy_Jr." title="Robert F. Kennedy Jr.">Robert F. Kennedy Jr.</a> stated that his father was "fairly convinced" that others besides Oswald were involved in his brother's assassination and that he privately believed the Commission's report was a "shoddy piece of craftsmanship".<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The killing was judged as having a profound impact on Kennedy. Beran assesses the assassination as having moved Kennedy away from reliance on the political system and become more questioning.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Tye views Kennedy following the death of his brother as "more fatalistic, having seen how fast he could lose what he cherished the most."<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice_presidential_candidate">Vice presidential candidate</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy;_sign_in_background_reads,_in_part,_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy%3B_sign_in_background_reads%2C_in_part%2C_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg/220px-David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy%3B_sign_in_background_reads%2C_in_part%2C_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy%3B_sign_in_background_reads%2C_in_part%2C_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg/330px-David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy%3B_sign_in_background_reads%2C_in_part%2C_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy%3B_sign_in_background_reads%2C_in_part%2C_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg/440px-David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy%3B_sign_in_background_reads%2C_in_part%2C_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1699" data-file-height="2131" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Dubinsky_with_Robert_F._Kennedy;_sign_in_background_reads,_in_part,_%22For_President-_Lyndon_B._Johnson.%22.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy with <a href="/wiki/David_Dubinsky" title="David Dubinsky">David Dubinsky</a> in an undated photo. The sign in the background reads, "For President – Lyndon B. Johnson".</div></div></div>
<p>In the wake of the assassination of his brother and Lyndon Johnson's ascension to the presidency, with the office of vice president now vacant, Kennedy was viewed favorably as a potential candidate for the position in the 1964 presidential election. Several Kennedy partisans called for him to be drafted in tribute to his brother; national polling showing that three of four Democrats were in favor of him as Johnson's running mate. Democratic organizers supported him as a write-in candidate in the New Hampshire primary and 25,000 Democrats wrote in Kennedy's name in March 1964, only 3,700 fewer than the number of Democrats who wrote in Johnson's name as their pick for president.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmero1-6_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmero1-6-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy discussed the vice presidency with Arthur Schlesinger. Schlesinger thought that he should develop his own political base first, and Kennedy observed that the job "was really based on waiting around for someone to die". In his first interview after the assassination Kennedy said he was not considering the vice presidency. During this time he said of the coalescing Johnson administration, "It's too early for me to even think about '64, because I don't know whether I want to have any part of these people. ... if they don't fulfill and follow out my brother's program, I don't want to have anything to do with them."<sup id="cite_ref-Bohrer2017_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohrer2017-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> However, in January 1964 Kennedy did begin low key inquiries as to the vice-presidential position and by the summer was developing plans to help Johnson in cities and in the Northeast based on the 1960 JFK campaign strategies.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the fanfare within the Democratic Party, President Johnson was not inclined to have Kennedy on his ticket. The two men disliked one another intensely, with feelings often described as "mutual contempt" that went back to their first meeting in 1953 and had only intensified during JFK's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Johnson instead chose <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a> to be his running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmero1-6_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmero1-6-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During a post-presidency interview with historian <a href="/wiki/Doris_Kearns_Goodwin" title="Doris Kearns Goodwin">Doris Kearns Goodwin</a>, Johnson claimed that Kennedy "acted like he was the custodian of the Kennedy dream" despite Johnson being seen as this after JFK was assassinated, arguing that he had "waited" his turn and Kennedy should have done the same. Johnson recalled a "tidal wave of letters and memos about how great a vice president Bobby would be" being swept upon him, but knowing that he could not "let it happen" as he viewed the possibility of having Kennedy on the ticket as ensuring that he would never know if he could be elected "on my own".<sup id="cite_ref-Sabato269_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sabato269-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 1964, Johnson issued an official statement ruling out all of his current cabinet members as potential running mates, judging them to be "so valuable ... in their current posts". In response to this statement, angry letters poured in directed towards both Johnson and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Lady_Bird_Johnson" title="Lady Bird Johnson">Lady Bird</a>, expressing disappointment at Kennedy being dropped from the field of potential running mates.<sup id="cite_ref-Sabato269_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sabato269-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Johnson, worried that delegates at the convention would draft Kennedy onto the ticket, ordered the FBI to monitor Kennedy's contacts, actions, and to make sure that he could not speak until after Hubert Humphrey was confirmed as his running mate.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmero1-6_148-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmero1-6-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="U.S._Senate_.281965.E2.80.931968.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Senate_(1965–1968)">U.S. Senate (1965–1968)</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1964_election">1964 election</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_New_York,_1964" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate election in New York, 1964">United States Senate election in New York, 1964</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="649" data-file-height="978" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Kennedy_at_the_1964_Democratic_National_Convention_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy at the 1964 Democratic National Convention</div></div></div>
<p>Nine months after his brother's assassination, Kennedy left the cabinet to run for a seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senate</a> representing New York,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> announcing his candidacy on August 22, 1964, two days before that year's Democratic National Convention. He had considered the possibility of running since early spring, but also giving consideration to leaving politics altogether after the plane crash and injury of his brother Ted in June, two months earlier. Positive reception in Europe convinced him to remain in politics.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was lauded during trips to Germany and Poland, the denizens of the latter country's greetings to Kennedy being interpreted by Leaming as evaporating the agony he had sustained since his brother's passing.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaming178_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaming178-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy was given permission to run by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Democratic_Committee" title="New York State Democratic Committee">New York State Democratic Committee</a> on September 1, amid mixed feelings in regards to his candidacy.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
Despite their notoriously difficult relationship, Johnson gave considerable support to Kennedy's campaign. His opponent in the <a href="/wiki/1964_United_States_Senate_elections" title="1964 United States Senate elections">1964 race</a> was <a href="/wiki/United_States_Republican_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Republican Party">Republican</a> incumbent <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Keating" title="Kenneth Keating">Kenneth Keating</a>, who attempted to portray Kennedy as an arrogant <a href="/wiki/Carpetbagger" title="Carpetbagger">carpetbagger</a> from Massachusetts.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> RFK charged Keating with having "not done much of anything constructive" despite his presence in Congress during a September 8 press conference.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy won the November election, helped in part by Johnson's huge victory margin in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaming189_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaming189-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenure">Tenure</span></h3>
<p>Kennedy drew attention in Congress early on as the brother of President Kennedy, which set him apart from other senators. He drew more than fifty senators as spectators when he delivered a speech in the Senate on nuclear proliferation in June 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> However, he also saw a decline in his power, going from the president's most trusted advisor to one of a hundred senators, and his impatience with collaborative lawmaking showed.<sup id="cite_ref-Canellos_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canellos-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Though fellow senator <a href="/wiki/Fred_R._Harris" title="Fred R. Harris">Fred R. Harris</a> expected not to like Kennedy, the two became allies; Harris even called them "each other's best friends in the Senate".<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy's younger brother Ted was his senior there. Robert saw his brother as a guide on managing within the Senate and the arrangement worked to deepen their relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-Canellos_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canellos-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Senator Harris noted that Kennedy was intense about matters and issues which concerned him.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger,_682,_683_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger,_682,_683-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy gained a reputation in the Senate for being well prepared for debate, however his tendency to speak to other senators in a more "blunt" fashion caused him to be "unpopular ... with many of his colleagues".<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger,_682,_683_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger,_682,_683-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg/240px-Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg/360px-Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg/480px-Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1345" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Immigration_Bill_Signing_-_A1421-33a_-_10-03-1965.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President Lyndon B. Johnson signs the <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Act_of_1965" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration Act of 1965">Immigration Act of 1965</a> as Ted and Robert Kennedy and others look on</div></div></div>
<p>While serving in the Senate, Kennedy advocated <a href="/wiki/Gun_control" title="Gun control">gun control</a>. In May 1965 he co-sponsored S.1592, proposed by President Johnson and sponsored by Senator <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Dodd" title="Thomas J. Dodd">Thomas J. Dodd</a>, that would put federal restrictions on mail-order gun sales.<sup id="cite_ref-cq_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Speaking in support of the bill, Kennedy said, "For too long we dealt with these deadly weapons as if they were harmless toys. Yet their very presence, the ease of their acquisition and the familiarity of their appearance have led to thousands of deaths each year. With the passage of this bill we will begin to meet our responsibilities. It would save hundreds of thousands of lives in this country and spare thousands of families ... grief and heartache. ... "<sup id="cite_ref-cq_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cq-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> In remarks during a May 1968 campaign stop in <a href="/wiki/Roseburg,_Oregon" title="Roseburg, Oregon">Roseburg, Oregon</a>, Kennedy defended the bill as keeping firearms away from "people who have no business with guns or rifles". The bill forbade "mail order sale of guns to the very young, those with criminal records and the insane," according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Oregonian" title="The Oregonian">The Oregonian</a>'</i>s report.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> S.1592 and subsequent bills, and the assassinations of Martin Luther King Jr. and Robert F. Kennedy, paved the way for the eventual passage of the <a href="/wiki/Gun_Control_Act_of_1968" title="Gun Control Act of 1968">Gun Control Act of 1968</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilson2007_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2007-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy and his staff had employed a cautionary "amendments–only" strategy for his first year in the senate. In 1966 and 1967 they took more direct legislative action, but were met with increasing resistance from the Johnson administration.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Despite perceptions that the two were hostile in their respective offices to each other, <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. News">U.S. News</a></i> reported Kennedy's support of the Johnson administration's "<a href="/wiki/Great_Society" title="Great Society">Great Society</a>" program through his voting record. Kennedy supported both major and minor parts of the program, and each year over 60% of his roll call votes were consistently in favor of Johnson's policies.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 8, 1966, Kennedy urged the United States to pledge that it would not be the first country to use nuclear weapons against countries that did not have them noting that China had made the pledge and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> indicated it was also willing to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 1966, he visited <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa_in_the_apartheid_era" class="mw-redirect" title="History of South Africa in the apartheid era">apartheid-era South Africa</a> accompanied by his wife, Ethel, and a few aides. The tour was greeted with international praise at a time when few politicians dared to entangle themselves in the politics of South Africa. He spoke out against the oppression of the native population, and was welcomed by the black population as though he were a visiting head of state. In an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Look_(American_magazine)" title="Look (American magazine)">Look</a></i> magazine he said:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>At the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Natal" title="University of Natal">University of Natal</a> in <a href="/wiki/Durban" title="Durban">Durban</a>, I was told the church to which most of the white population belongs teaches apartheid as a moral necessity. A questioner declared that few churches allow black Africans to pray with the white because the Bible says that is the way it should be, because God created Negroes to serve. 'But suppose God is black', I replied. 'What if we go to Heaven and we, all our lives, have treated the Negro as an inferior, and God is there, and we look up and He is not white? What then is our response?' There was no answer. Only silence.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cape_Town" title="University of Cape Town">University of Cape Town</a> he delivered the annual <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Affirmation_Address" title="Day of Affirmation Address">Day of Affirmation Address</a>. A quote from this address appears on his memorial at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>: "Each time a man stands up for an ideal, or acts to improve the lot of others, or strikes out against injustice, he sends forth a tiny ripple of hope."<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 28, 1967, Kennedy began a ten-day stay in Europe, meeting <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> and advising him to tell President Johnson about his belief that the ongoing Vietnam conflict was wrong. Upon returning to the U.S. in early February, he was confronted by the press who asked him if his conversations abroad had negatively impacted American foreign relations.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg/220px-Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg/330px-Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg/440px-Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg 2x" data-file-width="684" data-file-height="898" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_discusses_school_with_young_Ricky_Taggart.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy speaks with a youth while touring Bedford–Stuyvesant in Brooklyn, N.Y., February 1966</div></div></div>
<p>During his years as a senator, he helped to start <a href="/wiki/Bedford_Stuyvesant_Restoration_Corporation" title="Bedford Stuyvesant Restoration Corporation">a successful redevelopment project</a> in poverty-stricken <a href="/wiki/Bedford%E2%80%93Stuyvesant,_Brooklyn" title="Bedford–Stuyvesant, Brooklyn">Bedford–Stuyvesant, Brooklyn</a>, in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Schlesinger wrote that Kennedy had hoped Bedford-Stuyvesant would become an example of self-imposed growth for other impoverished neighborhoods. Kennedy had difficulty securing support from President Johnson, whose administration was charged by Kennedy as having opposed a "special impact" program meant to bring about the federal progress that he had supported. <a href="/wiki/Robert_B._Semple_Jr." title="Robert B. Semple Jr.">Robert B. Semple Jr.</a> repeated similar sentiments in September 1967, writing the Johnson administration was preparing "a concentrated attack" on Robert F. Kennedy's proposal that Semple claimed would "build more and better low-cost housing in the slums through private enterprise." Kennedy confided to journalist <a href="/wiki/Jack_Newfield" title="Jack Newfield">Jack Newfield</a> that while he tried collaborating with the administration through courting its members and compromising with the bill, "They didn't even try to work something out together. To them it's all just politics."<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy,_sem_data.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy%2C_sem_data.tif/lossy-page1-175px-Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy%2C_sem_data.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="175" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy%2C_sem_data.tif/lossy-page1-263px-Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy%2C_sem_data.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy%2C_sem_data.tif/lossy-page1-350px-Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy%2C_sem_data.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4604" data-file-height="5581" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pel%C3%A9_e_Robert_Kennedy,_sem_data.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pel%C3%A9" title="Pelé">Pelé</a> and Kennedy shaking hands after a game at <a href="/wiki/Maracan%C3%A3_Stadium" title="Maracanã Stadium">Maracanã Stadium</a></div></div></div>
<p>He also <a href="/wiki/Joseph_S._Clark%27s_and_Robert_F._Kennedy%27s_tour_of_the_Mississippi_Delta" title="Joseph S. Clark&#39;s and Robert F. Kennedy&#39;s tour of the Mississippi Delta">visited</a> the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a> as a member of the Senate committee reviewing the effectiveness of 'War on Poverty' programs, particularly that of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Opportunity_Act_of_1964" title="Economic Opportunity Act of 1964">Economic Opportunity Act of 1964</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marian_Wright_Edelman" title="Marian Wright Edelman">Marian Wright Edelman</a> described Kennedy as "deeply moved and outraged" by the sight of the starving children living in the economically abysmal climate, changing her impression of him from "tough, arrogant, and politically driven."<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Edelman noted further that the senator requested she call on Martin Luther King Jr. to bring the impoverished to Washington, D.C., to make them more visible, leading to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Poor_People%27s_Campaign" title="Poor People&#39;s Campaign">Poor People's Campaign</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy worked on the <a href="/wiki/Senate_Labor_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate Labor Committee">Senate Labor Committee</a> at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Workers&#39; rights">workers' rights</a> activism of <a href="/wiki/Cesar_Chavez" title="Cesar Chavez">Cesar Chavez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolores_Huerta" title="Dolores Huerta">Dolores Huerta</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Farm_Workers_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="National Farm Workers Association">National Farm Workers Association</a> (NFWA).<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> At the request of labor leader Walter Reuther, who had previously marched with and provided money to Chavez, Kennedy flew out to <a href="/wiki/Delano,_California" title="Delano, California">Delano, California</a>, to investigate the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Although little attention was paid to the first two committee hearings in March 1966 for legislation to include farm workers by an amendment of the <a href="/wiki/National_Labor_Relations_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="National Labor Relations Act">National Labor Relations Act</a>, Kennedy's attendance at the third hearing brought media coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Biographer Thomas wrote that Kennedy was moved after seeing the conditions of the workers, who he deemed were being taken advantage of.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Chavez stressed to Kennedy that migrant workers needed to be recognized as human beings. Kennedy later engaged in an exchange with <a href="/wiki/Kern_County" class="mw-redirect" title="Kern County">Kern County</a> sheriff Leroy Galyen where he criticized the sheriff's deputies for taking photographs of "people on picket lines."<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a senator, he was popular among African Americans and other minorities including Native Americans and immigrant groups. He spoke forcefully in favor of what he called the "disaffected",<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> the impoverished,<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> and "the excluded",<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> thereby aligning himself with leaders of the civil rights struggle and social justice campaigners, leading the Democratic party in pursuit of a more aggressive agenda to eliminate perceived discrimination on all levels. He supported <a href="/wiki/Desegregation_busing" title="Desegregation busing">desegregation busing</a>, integration of all public facilities, the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>, and anti-poverty social programs to increase education, offer opportunities for employment, and provide health care for African Americans. Consistent with President Kennedy's <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Progress" title="Alliance for Progress">Alliance for Progress</a>, he also placed increasing emphasis on human rights as a central focus of U.S. foreign policy.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span></h4>
<p>The JFK administration had backed U.S. involvement in Southeast Asia and other parts of the world in the frame of the Cold War, but Kennedy was not known to be involved in discussions on the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> when he was his brother's attorney general.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> According to historian <a href="/wiki/Doris_Kearns_Goodwin" title="Doris Kearns Goodwin">Doris Kearns Goodwin</a>, before choosing to run for the Senate, Kennedy had sought an ambassadorship to <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Entering the Senate, Kennedy initially kept private his disagreements with President Johnson on the war. While Kennedy vigorously supported his brother's earlier efforts, he never publicly advocated commitment of ground troops. Though bothered by the beginning of the bombing of <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a> in February 1965, Kennedy did not wish to appear antipathetic to the president's agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills359_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills359-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> But by April, Kennedy was advocating a halt to the bombing to Johnson, who acknowledged that Kennedy played a part in influencing his choice to temporarily cease bombing the following month.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy cautioned Johnson against sending combat troops as early as 1965, but Johnson chose instead to follow the recommendation of the rest of his predecessor's still intact staff of advisers. In July, after Johnson made a large commitment of American ground forces to Vietnam, Kennedy made multiple calls for a settlement through negotiation. The next month, <a href="/wiki/John_Paul_Vann" title="John Paul Vann">John Paul Vann</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_colonel" title="Lieutenant colonel">lieutenant colonel</a> in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Army" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a>, wrote that Kennedy "indicat[ed] comprehension of the problems we face", in a letter to the senator.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif/lossy-page1-240px-Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif/lossy-page1-360px-Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif/lossy-page1-480px-Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1966" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Senator_Robert_F._Kennedy_and_President_Lyndon_B._Johnson_-_NARA_-_192487.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Senator Robert F. Kennedy and President Lyndon B. Johnson in the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a>, 1966</div></div></div>
<p>In April 1966, Kennedy had a private meeting with <a href="/wiki/Philip_Heymann" title="Philip Heymann">Philip Heymann</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department's</a> Bureau of Security and Consular Affairs to discuss efforts to secure the release of American prisoners of war in Vietnam. Kennedy wanted to press the Johnson administration to do more, but Heymann insisted that the administration believed the "consequences of sitting down with the Viet Cong" mattered more than the prisoners they were holding captive.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> On June 29 of that year, Kennedy released a statement disavowing President Johnson's choice to bomb <a href="/wiki/Haiphong" title="Haiphong">Haiphong</a>, but he avoided criticizing either the war or the president's overall foreign policy, believing that it might harm Democratic candidates in the 1966 <a href="/wiki/Midterm_election" title="Midterm election">midterm elections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> In August, the <i><a href="/wiki/International_Herald_Tribune" class="mw-redirect" title="International Herald Tribune">International Herald Tribune</a></i> described Kennedy's popularity as outpacing President Johnson's, crediting Kennedy's attempts to end the Vietnam conflict which the public increasingly desired.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTAugust2016_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTAugust2016-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early part of 1967, Kennedy traveled to Europe, where he had discussions about Vietnam with leaders and diplomats. A story leaked to the State Department that Kennedy was talking about seeking peace while President Johnson was pursuing the war. Johnson became convinced that Kennedy was undermining his authority. He voiced this during a meeting with Kennedy, who reiterated the interest of the European leaders to pause the bombing while going forward with negotiations; Johnson declined to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> On March 2, Kennedy outlined a three-point plan to end the war which included suspending the U.S. bombing of North Vietnam, and the eventual withdrawal of American and North Vietnamese soldiers from South Vietnam; this plan was rejected by <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Dean_Rusk" title="Dean Rusk">Dean Rusk</a>, who believed North Vietnam would never agree to it.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> On May 15, Kennedy debated <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_California" title="Governor of California">Governor of California</a> <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> about the war.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> On November 26, 1967, during an appearance on <i><a href="/wiki/Face_the_Nation" title="Face the Nation">Face the Nation</a></i>, Kennedy asserted that the Johnson administration had deviated from his brother's policies in Vietnam, his first time contrasting the two administrations' policies on the war. He added that the view that Americans were fighting to end communism in Vietnam was "immoral".<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 8, 1968, Kennedy delivered an address in <a href="/wiki/Chicago,_Illinois" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago, Illinois">Chicago, Illinois</a>, where he critiqued <a href="/wiki/Saigon" class="mw-redirect" title="Saigon">Saigon</a> "government corruption" and expressed his disagreement with the Johnson administration's stance that the war would determine the future of Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> On March 14, Kennedy met with defense secretary <a href="/wiki/Clark_Clifford" title="Clark Clifford">Clark Clifford</a> at the <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">Pentagon</a> regarding the war. Clifford's notes indicate that Kennedy was offering not to enter the ongoing Democratic presidential primary if President Johnson would admit publicly to having been wrong in his war policy and appoint "a group of persons to conduct a study in depth of the issues and come up with a recommended course of action";<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Johnson rejected the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> On April 1, after President Johnson halted bombing of North Vietnam, RFK said the decision was a "step toward peace" and though offering to collaborate with Johnson for national unity, opted to continue his presidential bid.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> On May 1, while in <a href="/wiki/Lafayette,_Indiana" title="Lafayette, Indiana">Lafayette, Indiana</a>, Kennedy said continued delays in beginning peace talks with North Vietnam meant both more lives lost and the postponing of the "domestic progress" hoped for by the US.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Later that month, Kennedy called the war "the gravest kind of error" in a speech in Corvallis, Oregon.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> In an interview on June 4, hours before he was shot, Kennedy continued to advocate for a change in policy towards the war.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite his criticism of the Vietnam War and the South Vietnam government, Kennedy also stated in his 1968 campaign brochure that he did not support either a simple withdrawal or a surrender in South Vietnam and favored instead a change in the course of action taken so it would bring an "honorable peace."<sup id="cite_ref-uoooav_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uoooav-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_candidate">Presidential candidate</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy_1968_presidential_campaign" title="Robert F. Kennedy 1968 presidential campaign">Robert F. Kennedy 1968 presidential campaign</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/1968_United_States_presidential_election" title="1968 United States presidential election">1968 United States presidential election</a> and <a href="/wiki/1968_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1968 Democratic National Convention">1968 Democratic National Convention</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg/220px-RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg/330px-RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg/440px-RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RWRMay1968RFKspeaksm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tired but still intense in the last days before his Oregon defeat, Robert Kennedy speaks from the platform of a campaign train.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1968 President Johnson prepared to run for re-election. In January, faced with what was widely considered an unrealistic race against an incumbent president, Kennedy stated that he would not seek the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Last_Good_Campaign_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Last_Good_Campaign-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Tet_Offensive" title="Tet Offensive">Tet Offensive</a> in Vietnam in early February 1968, he received a letter from writer <a href="/wiki/Pete_Hamill" title="Pete Hamill">Pete Hamill</a> that said poor people kept pictures of President Kennedy on their walls and that Kennedy had an "obligation of staying true to whatever it was that put those pictures on those walls."<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy traveled to <a href="/wiki/Delano,_California" title="Delano, California">Delano, California</a>, to meet with civil rights activist <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9sar_Ch%C3%A1vez" class="mw-redirect" title="César Chávez">César Chávez</a>, who was on a 25-day hunger strike showing his commitment to <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">nonviolence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> It was on this visit to California that Kennedy decided he would challenge Johnson for the presidency, telling his former Justice Department aides, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Guthman" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin Guthman">Edwin Guthman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Edelman" title="Peter Edelman">Peter Edelman</a>, that his first step was to get lesser-known Senator <a href="/wiki/Eugene_McCarthy" title="Eugene McCarthy">Eugene McCarthy</a> of Minnesota to drop out of the presidential race.<sup id="cite_ref-Thurston,_2008_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thurston,_2008-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The weekend before the New Hampshire primary, Kennedy announced to several aides that he would attempt to persuade McCarthy to withdraw from the race to avoid splitting the antiwar vote, but Senator <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a> urged Kennedy to wait until after that primary to announce his candidacy.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Last_Good_Campaign_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Last_Good_Campaign-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Johnson won a narrow victory in the New Hampshire primary on March 12, 1968, against McCarthy, but this close second-place result dramatically boosted McCarthy's standing in the race.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After much speculation, and reports leaking out about his plans,<sup id="cite_ref-decides_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decides-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> and seeing in McCarthy's success that Johnson's hold on the job was not as strong as originally thought, Kennedy declared his candidacy on March 16, 1968, in the Caucus Room of the old Senate office building, the same room where his brother had declared his own candidacy eight years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-caucus_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caucus-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> He stated, "I do not run for the presidency merely to oppose any man, but to propose new policies. I run because I am convinced that this country is on a perilous course and because I have such strong feelings about what must be done, and I feel that I'm obliged to do all I can."<sup id="cite_ref-announcement_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-announcement-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>McCarthy supporters angrily denounced Kennedy as an opportunist. They believed that McCarthy had taken the most courageous stand by opposing the sitting president of his own party and that his surprising result in New Hampshire had earned him the mantle of being the anti-war candidate. Kennedy's announcement split the anti-war movement in two.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> On March 31, 1968, Johnson stunned the nation by dropping out of the race. Vice President <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a>, a champion of the labor unions and a long supporter of civil rights, entered the race with the financial backing and critical endorsement of the party "establishment", including most members of Congress, mayors, governors, "the south", and several major labor unions.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> With state registration deadlines long past, Humphrey joined the race too late to enter any primaries but had the support of the president.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy, like his brother before him, planned to win the nomination through popular support in the primaries.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rkennedy01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Rkennedy01.jpg/190px-Rkennedy01.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Rkennedy01.jpg/285px-Rkennedy01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Rkennedy01.jpg/380px-Rkennedy01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2555" data-file-height="2547" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rkennedy01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert Kennedy campaigns in Los Angeles (photo by <a href="/wiki/Evan_Freed" title="Evan Freed">Evan Freed</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Kennedy ran on a platform of racial and economic justice, non-aggression in foreign policy, decentralization of power, and social change. A crucial element of his campaign was an engagement with the young, whom he identified as being the future of a reinvigorated American society based on partnership and equality. His policy objectives did not sit well with the business community, where he was viewed as something of a fiscal liability, opposed as they were to the tax increases necessary to fund social programs. At one of his university speeches (Indiana University Medical School), he was asked, "Where are we going to get the money to pay for all these new programs you're proposing?" He replied to the medical students, about to enter lucrative careers, "From you."<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was this intense and frank mode of dialogue with which he was to continue to engage those whom he viewed as not being traditional allies of Democratic ideals or initiatives. In a speech at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">University of Alabama</a>, he argued, "I believe that any who seek high office this year must go before all Americans, not just those who agree with them, but also those who disagree, recognizing that it is not just our supporters, not just those who vote for us, but all Americans who we must lead in the difficult years ahead."<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> He aroused rabid animosity in some quarters, with J. Edgar Hoover's Deputy <a href="/wiki/Clyde_Tolson" title="Clyde Tolson">Clyde Tolson</a> reported as saying, "I hope that someone shoots and kills the son of a bitch."<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy's presidential campaign brought out both "great enthusiasm" and anger in people. His message of change raised hope for some and brought fear to others. Kennedy wanted to be a bridge across the divide of American society. His bid for the presidency saw not only a continuation of the programs he and his brother had undertaken during the president's term in office, but also an extension of Johnson's Great Society.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger_2002_1978,_p._xvi_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger_2002_1978,_p._xvi-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy visited numerous small towns and made himself available to the masses by participating in long motorcades and street-corner stump speeches, often in troubled inner cities. He made urban poverty a chief concern of his campaign, which in part led to enormous crowds that would attend his events in poor urban areas or rural parts of <a href="/wiki/Appalachia" title="Appalachia">Appalachia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RFK_speech_on_MLK.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/7a/RFK_speech_on_MLK.png/250px-RFK_speech_on_MLK.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/7a/RFK_speech_on_MLK.png 1.5x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RFK_speech_on_MLK.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kennedy giving his <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy%27s_speech_on_the_assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Robert F. Kennedy&#39;s speech on the assassination of Martin Luther King Jr.">speech on Martin Luther King Jr.</a></div></div></div>
<p>On April 4, 1968, Kennedy learned of the assassination of <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> and gave a heartfelt <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy%27s_speech_on_the_assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Robert F. Kennedy&#39;s speech on the assassination of Martin Luther King Jr.">impromptu speech</a> in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis,_Indiana" class="mw-redirect" title="Indianapolis, Indiana">Indianapolis's</a> inner city, calling for a reconciliation between the races. The address was the first time Kennedy spoke publicly about his brother's killing.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/King_assassination_riots" title="King assassination riots">Riots broke out in 60 cities in the wake of King's death</a>, but not in Indianapolis, a fact many attribute to the effect of this speech.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy addressed the <a href="/wiki/City_Club_of_Cleveland" title="City Club of Cleveland">City Club of Cleveland</a> the next day, on April 5, 1968, delivering the famous <a href="/wiki/On_the_Mindless_Menace_of_Violence" title="On the Mindless Menace of Violence">On the Mindless Menace of Violence</a> speech.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> He attended King's funeral, accompanied by Jacqueline and Ted Kennedy. He was described as being the "only white politician to hear only cheers and applause."<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kennedy won the <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> Democratic primary on May 7 and the Nebraska primary on May 14 but lost the Oregon primary to McCarthy on May 28.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> If he could defeat McCarthy in the California primary, the leadership of the campaign thought, he would knock McCarthy out of the race and set up a one-on-one against Hubert Humphrey at the <a href="/wiki/1968_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1968 Democratic National Convention">Chicago national convention</a> in August.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination">Assassination</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of Robert F. Kennedy">Assassination of Robert F. Kennedy</a></div>
<p>Kennedy scored a major victory when he won the California primary. He addressed his supporters shortly after midnight on June 5, 1968, in a <a href="/wiki/Ballroom" title="Ballroom">ballroom</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ambassador_Hotel_(Los_Angeles)" title="Ambassador Hotel (Los Angeles)">The Ambassador Hotel</a> in Los Angeles, California.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTjune61968_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTjune61968-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Leaving the ballroom, he went through the hotel kitchen after being told it was a shortcut to a press room.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> He did this despite being advised by his bodyguard – former FBI agent Bill Barry – to avoid the kitchen. In a crowded kitchen passageway, Kennedy turned to his left and shook hands with hotel <a href="/wiki/Busboy" class="mw-redirect" title="Busboy">busboy</a> Juan Romero just as <a href="/wiki/Sirhan_Sirhan" title="Sirhan Sirhan">Sirhan Sirhan</a>, a 24-year-old Palestinian,<sup id="cite_ref-NYTsirhan_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTsirhan-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> opened fire with a <a href="/wiki/.22_Short" title=".22 Short">.22-caliber</a> <a href="/wiki/Revolver" title="Revolver">revolver</a>. Kennedy was hit three times, and five other people were wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-cnnMartinez_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnnMartinez-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Plimpton" title="George Plimpton">George Plimpton</a>, former <a href="/wiki/Decathlon" title="Decathlon">decathlete</a> <a href="/wiki/Rafer_Johnson" title="Rafer Johnson">Rafer Johnson</a>, and former professional football player <a href="/wiki/Rosey_Grier" title="Rosey Grier">Rosey Grier</a> are credited with wrestling Sirhan to the ground after he shot the senator.<sup id="cite_ref-timealife_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timealife-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> As Kennedy lay mortally wounded, Romero cradled his head and placed a <a href="/wiki/Rosary" title="Rosary">rosary</a> in his hand. Kennedy asked Romero, "Is everybody OK?", and Romero responded, "Yes, everybody's OK." Kennedy then turned away from Romero and said, "Everything's going to be OK."<sup id="cite_ref-nw29-30_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nw29-30-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> After several minutes, medical attendants arrived and lifted the senator onto a stretcher, prompting him to whisper, "Don't lift me", which were his last words.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> He lost consciousness shortly thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-w273_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w273-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> He was rushed first to Los Angeles' Central Receiving Hospital, less than 2 miles (3.2&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/LAPD_Rampart_Division#History_of_Rampart_Division" title="LAPD Rampart Division">east of the Ambassador Hotel</a>, and then to the adjoining (one city block distant) <a href="/wiki/Good_Samaritan_Hospital_(Los_Angeles)" title="Good Samaritan Hospital (Los Angeles)">Good Samaritan Hospital</a>. Despite extensive neurosurgery to remove the bullet and bone fragments from his brain, Kennedy was pronounced dead at 1:44&#160;a.m. (PDT) on June 6, nearly 26 hours after the shooting.
</p><p>Robert Kennedy's death, like the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">1963 assassination of his brother, President John F. Kennedy</a>, has been the subject of <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy_assassination_conspiracy_theories" title="Robert F. Kennedy assassination conspiracy theories">conspiracy theories</a>.
</p>
